Third Objection: Verse Specific to Disbelievers
Author argues a verse about tasting the fire is specific to disbelievers who denied resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the third objection by contextualizing the verse and identifying a logical flaw in its use as proof of eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment verses target disbelievers who deny resurrection, not all sinners.

The third objection cites a verse threatening tasting the fire as evidence of eternal punishment. The author responds by showing the verse is explicitly addressed to disbelievers who denied the resurrection. Even if it implied eternity, the reasoning is flawed because the punishment could cease if the damned lose hope or become stupefied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and response
Read the passage and note that the author is responding to a third objection that uses a verse about tasting the fire to argue for eternal punishment.
The response has two parts: verse context and a logical critique.
2
Analyze the verse context
The author argues the verse is specifically for disbelievers who deny resurrection, citing the phrase 'which you used to deny'. Understand how this limits its applicability.
This shows the verse does not address believing sinners.
3
Examine the logical flaw
Even if the verse implied eternity, the author points out a manifest discussion: the punished might not intend to leave due to despair or stupefaction, so their remaining does not prove eternity.
This weakens the objection's proof of eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثالثة بأنها في حق الكافرين المنكرين للحشر بقرينة قوله : ( ذوقوا وعن عذاب النار الذي كنتم به تكذبون (۳) . مع ما في دلالتها على الخلود من المناقشة الظاهرة، لجواز أن يخرجوا عند عدم إرادتهم الخروج باليأس أو الذهول، أو نحو ذلك .
And concerning the third (objection): that it is regarding the disbelievers who deny the resurrection, by the indication of His saying: 'Taste and (avoid?) the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' (3). Along with what is in its indication of eternity of manifest discussion, because it is possible that they exit when they do not intend to exit due to despair or stupefaction, or similar.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection, which likely cites a verse about tasting the fire (As-Sajdah 20). The author argues that this verse is specifically about disbelievers who deny the resurrection, as indicated by the phrase 'which you used to deny'. Therefore it does not apply to believers who commit major sins. Furthermore, even if the verse implied eternity, there is a manifest flaw in its reasoning: the punishment could be interrupted if the disbelievers lose hope or become stupefied and thus do not desire to leave. This shows that the verse cannot be taken as proof of eternal punishment.

Key Terms
الحشر (al-hashr)
الكافرين (al-kafireen)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verses threatening eternal punishment apply to all sinners including believers.
Correction: Such verses are specifically for disbelievers who deny the resurrection, as indicated by context. Believing sinners are not addressed by these verses.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the verse 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' is specifically about which group?
A) All major sinners
B) Disbelievers who deny resurrection
C) Hypocrites
D) Those who commit shirk

Key Takeaway
Verses threatening eternal punishment are specifically for disbelievers who deny the resurrection, not for believing sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about punishment, do you instinctively assume they apply only to 'true disbelievers' and not to yourself? How does this assumption affect your urgency to repent and correct your own shortcomings?
?
The author suggests that even disbelievers might exit punishment due to despair or stupefaction, implying that Allah's mercy can overcome even the most hopeless states. What does this tell you about your own hopelessness regarding your sins? Will you continue to despair, or will you act on that hope today?
?
If this verse is specifically for those who deny resurrection, then you—who affirm it—must live in a way that proves your belief is real. What specific action will you take this week to demonstrate that you truly believe you will stand before Allah on the Day of Resurrection?

Specifying the Verse to Disbelievers
Author resolves objection by specifying the verse to disbelievers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how apparent general verses can be specified to a particular group to reconcile conflicting evidence.

Core Idea
General verses can be specified by stronger evidence for harmony.

When two pieces of evidence seem to conflict, kalam scholars use the principle of specification (takhsis) to reconcile them. In this passage, the author addresses a verse that appears to state that no one will ever be absent from the Fire. He concedes its apparent generality but argues that it must be restricted to disbelievers only, based on other stronger evidences. Understanding this move is key to grasping how theodicy and eschatology are coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the concessive language: 'after conceding its indication... it is only specified to disbelievers.'
The verse in question is likely Al-Infitar 82:16.
2
Identify the author's concession
The author grants that the verse negates absence from the Fire for every individual and indicates perpetual absence. Identify these two concessions.
3
Understand the specification
Notice that despite this concession, the author restricts the verse's application to disbelievers. This is the key move: takhsis (specification).
Specification does not deny the verse's authority but limits its scope.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
Reflect on why specification is necessary: to harmonize conflicting evidences (jam' bayn al-adillah). This is a fundamental principle in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
Other evidences (e.g., hadith about believers exiting Hell) require this restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الرابعة بعد تسليم إفادتها النفي عن كل فرد ودلالتها على دوام عدم الغيبة إنما يخص بالكفار جمعاً بين الأدلة .
And concerning the fourth (objection), after conceding its indication of negation from every individual and its indication of perpetual absence from the Fire, it is only specified to the disbelievers to harmonize the evidences.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This brief passage addresses the fourth objection, likely based on the verse 'they will not be absent from it' (Al-Infitar 16). The author concedes that the verse appears to negate exit from the Fire for everyone it addresses, and indicates perpetual absence. However, he argues that this must be specified to apply only to disbelievers, in order to harmonize between this and other evidences that show believers can exit the Fire. This is a standard principle in kalam: when verses conflict, they are reconciled by specification (takhsis) based on stronger evidence.

Key Terms
الغيبة (al-ghaybah)
التخصيص (al-takhsis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A verse that appears general cannot be restricted to a specific group.
Correction: In Islamic legal theory, general verses can be specified (takhsis) based on stronger evidence, as done here with the disbelievers.

Checkpoint
How does the author reconcile the verse 'they will not be absent from it' with other evidences?
A) He denies the verse is authentic
B) He says it is abrogated
C) He specifies it to disbelievers only
D) He interprets 'absence' metaphorically

Key Takeaway
The principle of takhsis allows apparently general verses to be specified to particular groups when stronger evidence requires it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that seems to promise eternal punishment, do you immediately apply it to everyone, including believers, without considering other texts? How does this lesson challenge you to reconcile your interpretation?
?
Are you willing to let go of a literal understanding of a verse when stronger evidence demands it? What personal attachments to certain interpretations are you holding onto that might need specification?
?
The author specifies the verse to disbelievers for harmony. In your life, when do you choose harmony of understanding over strict literalism? Where do you need to specify your own limited perspective to align with the whole truth?

Refuting Scriptural Proofs for Eternal Punishment
Author responds to three scriptural objections on eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to three scriptural objections that claim certain verses prove eternal punishment for sinful believers, by contextualizing them to disbelievers or reinterpreting ambiguous terms.

Core Idea
Scriptural threats of eternal punishment are contextually limited, not universal.

Opponents of the view that sinful believers will eventually exit Hell often cite verses that seem to threaten eternal punishment. The author here responds to three such objections, showing that the verses refer specifically to disbelievers who deny the Resurrection, or that key terms like 'limits' and 'encompassing' must be understood in a way that excludes believers. This passage demonstrates the principle of harmonizing conflicting proofs by contextual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully to grasp the author's responses to each objection.
Notice the concise style; each response is brief.
2
Identify the three objections
The passage addresses the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth objections. Note how each is answered: the third by contextual evidence, the fourth by restriction to disbelievers, and the fifth/sixth by reinterpretation of terms.
3
Examine the rationale behind each response
Understand why each response works: for the third, the verse explicitly mentions denial of the Resurrection; for the fourth, harmonization requires restricting a general statement; for the fifth and sixth, ambiguous terms must be defined to fit the doctrine.
This shows the author's method of ta'wil (interpretive reconciliation).
4
Reflect on the role of probability
The author notes that the word 'khulud' itself is open to interpretation, so absolute eternity is not proven. Consider how this probabilistic argument weakens the opponent's case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة بأنها في حق الكافرين المنكرين للحشر بقرينة قوله : ( ذوقوا وعن عذاب النار الذي كنتم به تكذبون) . مع ما في دلالتها على الخلود من المناقشة الظاهرة، لجواز أن يخرجوا عند عدم إرادتهم الخروج باليأس أو الذهول، أو نحو ذلك . وعن الرابعة بعد تسليم إفادتها النفي عن كل فرد ودلالتها على دوام عدم الغيبة إنما يخص بالكفار جمعاً بين الأدلة . وكذا الخامسة والسادسة حملاً للحدود على حدود الإسلام، ولإحاطة الخطيئة على غلبتها ، بحيث لا يبقى معها الإيمان هذا مع ما في الخلود من الاحتمال.
The third (objection) [is answered] that it refers specifically to the disbelievers who deny the Resurrection, by the contextual evidence of His saying: ‘Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny’ (32:20). Moreover, its indication of khulud is subject to obvious critical discussion, because it is possible that they could exit when they do not intend to exit due to despair or forgetfulness, or the like. As for the fourth, after conceding that it negates [exit] from every individual and indicates perpetual non-exit, it is restricted to the disbelievers in order to reconcile the proofs. And similarly the fifth and sixth, [are answered] by interpreting the ‘limits’ as the limits of Islam, and ‘encompassing of sin’ as its dominance to the point that faith no longer remains with it. This, along with the probability in the word khulud.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the author's responses to three scriptural proofs (the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth) used by opponents to argue that sinful believers will remain in Hell forever. The third objection is answered by noting that the verse (32:20) addresses disbelievers who deny the Resurrection, as indicated by the phrase 'which you used to deny'. Even if the verse implies khulud, it is open to critique because the punishment might end when the sinners no longer intend to remain due to despair or forgetfulness—a scenario that cannot be ruled out. The fourth objection, which likely involves a verse negating any exit from Hell, is accepted as evidence of perpetual non-exit but only for disbelievers, harmonizing contradictory proofs. The fifth and sixth are refuted by interpreting ambiguous terms: 'limits' (hudūd) refers to the boundaries of Islam, and 'encompassing of sin' (iḥāṭat al-khaṭī’a) means sin dominates a person to the point that faith is no longer present, implying the person is effectively a disbeliever. Additionally, the word khulud itself is open to interpretation, so absolute eternity is not proven.

Key Terms
الخلود
الكافرون
المنكرون للحشر
الآية
الحدود
إحاطة الخطيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That scriptural threats of eternal punishment apply universally without contextual restrictions.
Correction: The author shows that verses are restricted to disbelievers or reinterpreted, so they do not apply to sinful believers.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the fourth objection about a verse that negates exit from Hell?
A) He denies the verse is authentic.
B) He accepts it but restricts its application to disbelievers.
C) He reinterprets 'exit' to mean a temporary leave.
D) He says it applies only to sinful believers.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural verses that seem to threaten eternal punishment must be interpreted contextually or restricted to disbelievers, preserving the doctrine that sinful believers will eventually exit Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'Taste the punishment of the Fire' (32:20), do you instinctively feel it applies to you, or do you stop to consider its context? How might this lesson change the way you approach threatening verses in the Quran? What specific verse in your life needs this kind of contextual understanding?
?
The author argues that the word 'khulud' (eternity) is open to interpretation. Are there Quranic concepts you have always taken for granted as absolute without examining their deeper meanings? Which one, and how can you begin to study it more carefully starting today?
?
This passage shows that scriptural threats are often restricted to specific groups. Does this give you a false sense of security, or does it drive you to examine your own faith more deeply? What would it mean for you to personally ensure that you are not among those described as 'disbelievers who deny the Resurrection'?

Refuting the Argument that Sinful Believers Enter Paradise by Merit
The author refutes the argument that a sinful believer's entrance to Paradise would require merit, which is impossible due to sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument against sinful believers entering Paradise by merit and the author's refutation based on God's grace.

Core Idea
Salvation hinges on God's grace, not human merit.

The author presents a syllogistic argument that would make the salvation of sinful believers impossible if merit were required. He then dismantles it by denying its premises and affirming that God's bounty, mercy, promise, and forgiveness are the true grounds for entry into Paradise. This passage clarifies that divine grace, not human deserving, is the foundation of hope for sinful believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by its denial.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues: If a sinful believer enters Paradise, it must be by merit. Why? Because no undeserving person enters. And merit is impossible for a sinner because of annulment or balancing. Trace each premise.
The terms iḥbāṭ and muwāzana are introduced but not explained here.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author refutes by denying both premises. He says entering Paradise is not by merit but by God's bounty and mercy. And even if merit were needed, it's not automatically voided. Focus on the positive assertion: 'rather, he enters by God's bounty...'
4
Reflect on the theological principle
The key takeaway is that salvation is ultimately a gift from God, not a wage earned. This aligns with Sunni belief in divine grace (faḍl) and mercy (raḥma).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن الفاسق لو دخل الجنة لكان باستحقاق لامتناع دخول غير المستحق كالكافر. واللازم منتف لبطلان الاستحقاق بالإحباط أو الموازنة على ما سيجيء. ورد بمنع المقدمتين، بل إنما يدخل بفضل الله ورحمته ووعده ومغفرته . وسنتكلم على الإحباط والموازنة .
The second [argument]: If the sinful believer entered Paradise, it would be by merit, because it is impossible for someone undeserving to enter, like the disbeliever. The consequent is false, because merit is invalidated by annulment (al-iḥbāṭ) or balancing (al-muwāzana), as will come. It is refuted by denying both premises; rather, he enters by God’s bounty, mercy, promise, and forgiveness. We will discuss al-iḥbāṭ and al-muwāzana later.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an argument against the possibility of sinful believers eventually entering Paradise. The argument claims that entering Paradise requires personal merit (istiḥqāq), since no undeserving person (like a disbeliever) can enter. Therefore, if a sinful believer goes to Paradise, he must have merit. But the opponent argues that such merit is nullified by sin through either annulment (iḥbāṭ) or balancing (muwāzana)—doctrines that will be explained later. Thus, the conclusion is false. The author refutes this by denying both premises: first, entering Paradise does not require personal merit; second, even if merit were needed, it is not necessarily nullified by sin. Instead, the sinful believer enters by God’s bounty (faḍl), mercy (raḥma), promise (wa‘d), and forgiveness (maghfira). The author postpones detailed discussion of iḥbāṭ and muwāzana.

Key Terms
الفساق
الإستحقاق
الإحباط
الموازنة
فضل الله
رحمة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A sinful believer cannot enter Paradise because his sin nullifies all his merit, making him undeserving.
Correction: The author explains that entry into Paradise is not by personal merit but by God's bounty, mercy, promise, and forgiveness, so the nullification of merit is irrelevant. Even if merit were considered, it is not necessarily voided; that will be discussed later.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does a sinful believer enter Paradise?
A) Through personal merit
B) Through God's bounty, mercy, promise, and forgiveness
C) Through the intercession of angels
D) Through balancing good and evil deeds

Key Takeaway
The salvation of sinful believers is not based on their own merit but on God's grace, mercy, promise, and forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, do you truly rely on God's grace for salvation, or do you secretly think your good deeds will tip the scale? What would it mean for you if salvation were purely by merit?
?
If you believed that your entrance to Paradise depends on your own merit, how would that change the way you view God's mercy? Are you willing to accept that you enter by His grace alone, even if that feels 'unfair'?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. Does this lesson challenge your assumption that it might disqualify you from Paradise? How does God's promise and forgiveness affect your hope?

Refuting Eternal Punishment as a Necessary Grace
The author refutes the argument that eternal punishment is a necessary grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that eternal punishment is a necessary divine grace for all people is rationally unsound, and how the author denies both the obligation of grace and its confinement to perpetuity.

Core Idea
Not every threat deters everyone; grace is not obligatory upon God.

Some theologians argued that eternal punishment must be applied to all sinners because it is the most effective deterrent—a divine grace (luṭf). The author of the passage refutes this by denying two premises: first, that such grace is obligatory upon God, and second, that it is confined to perpetual punishment. He also shows that the argument leads to absurd consequences, such as the insufficiency of even eternal fire.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the claim and the refutation.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the two denied premises
The author denies both the obligation of grace (wuṣūb al-luṭf) and its confinement to perpetuity. Find these in the text.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author points out that someone not deterred by ages in Hell will hardly be deterred by eternity. Reflect on why this undermines the argument.
4
Consider the logical consequence
If maximum deterrence were obligatory, then even eternal fire would be insufficient because a greater threat is possible. This shows the argument's absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن الوعيد بالعقاب الدائم لطف بالعباد لكونه أزجر على المعاصي. فإن منهم من لا يكترث بالعذاب المنقطع عند الميل إلى المستلذات ، ثم لا بد من تحقيق الوعيد تصديقاً للخبر وصوناً للقول عن التبديل. ورد بمنع وجوب اللطف ومنع إنحصاره في الدوام، فإن من لا يكترث باللبث في الجحيم أحقاباً ، قلما يستكثر الخلود فيها عقاباً، وإذ قد كان كل وعيد لطفاً، ولا شيء من الوعيد بلطف للكل فليكن لطف الخلود في النار مختصاً بالكفار، وكفى بوعيد النيران، بل وعد الجنات لطفاً ومزجرة لأهل الإيمان . ولو وجب ما هو الغاية في اللطف والزجر ، لما صح الاكتفاء بوعيد الخلود في النار لإمكان المزيد.
The fourth [argument]: The threat of perpetual punishment is a grace (luṭf) to the servants because it is a stronger deterrent against sins. For among them are those who are unmoved by temporary punishment when inclining to pleasures. Then it is necessary to fulfill the threat to verify the report and preserve the statement from alteration. It is refuted by denying the obligation of grace (wuṣūb al-luṭf) and denying that it is confined to perpetuity. For one who is not deterred by remaining in Hell for ages will hardly consider eternal punishment more severe. Moreover, since every threat is a grace, and no threat is a grace for everyone, let the grace of eternal punishment be specific to disbelievers. The threat of Hellfire is sufficient, indeed the promise of Gardens is a grace and deterrent for the people of faith. And if what is the ultimate in grace and deterrence were obligatory, then it would not be valid to be satisfied with the threat of eternal punishment in the Fire, because a greater [threat] is possible.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage refutes a utilitarian argument that eternal punishment is a divine grace (luṭf) that effectively deters people from sin, and therefore must be fulfilled for all sinners, including sinful believers, to maintain credibility. The author denies two premises: (1) that such grace is obligatory upon God (wuṣūb al-luṭf) and (2) that it is confined to perpetual punishment. He explains that a person who is not deterred by punishment lasting for ages (aḥqāb) will hardly be more deterred by eternal punishment; in fact, the perceived severity may not increase proportionally. Moreover, threats are graces only relative to specific individuals: the threat of eternal fire may be a grace for some but not for all. Hence, it is sufficient to apply it specifically to disbelievers, while believers are deterred by the promise of Paradise and lesser threats. Finally, if God were obliged to provide the maximum possible deterrent, then the threat of eternal fire would itself be insufficient, since an even more severe punishment could be conceived—an absurd implication. Thus, the argument fails.

Key Terms
الوعيد
العقاب الدائم
اللطف
الزجر
الكافرون
المؤمنون
الجنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to threaten the most severe punishment possible for all sinners to deter them.
Correction: The author argues that grace is not obligatory upon God, and that what deters varies among individuals; moreover, the argument leads to an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that eternal punishment is a necessary grace for all?
A) Because God does not care about deterrence
B) Because some people are not deterred even by eternal punishment, and grace is not obligatory
C) Because eternal punishment does not exist
D) Because believers do not sin

Key Takeaway
The claim that eternal punishment is a necessary grace is refuted by denying its obligation and its universal applicability, and by showing it leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself obeying God only because you fear Hellfire, and not out of love? This passage shows that even the threat of eternal punishment may not truly change a heart. What does that reveal about the state of your own faith? Will you commit today to checking your motives in every act of worship?
?
The author denies that God is obligated to give us grace. When you do good deeds, do you secretly feel that God owes you something in return? How can you begin to see every blessing—including the ability to obey—as a pure gift rather than a payment?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. Are you relying on fear of punishment to stop you, or is there a deeper love for God that makes the sin repulsive? If fear alone is your motivation, what would it take for you to desire Allah's pleasure more than you fear His displeasure? What will you change starting today?

The Mu'tazilite Scriptural Argument for Iḥbāṭ and the Ashʿarī Response
Ash'ari response to Mu'tazilite proof-texts for ihbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ashʿarīs reinterpret the Quranic verses about nullification of deeds to refute the Muʿtazilite doctrine of iḥbāṭ.

Core Idea
The Quran mentions nullification, but not in the Muʿtazilite sense of canceling prior rewards.

The Muʿtazilites claimed that the Quran explicitly supports their view that major sins nullify all good deeds. They cited verses about raised voices and invalid charity. The Ashʿarī response does not deny the verses but reinterprets them: they refer to deeds done in a blameworthy manner, not to the cancellation of prior rewards. This careful distinction preserves both scriptural authority and the principle that good deeds are not undone except by apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite citation and the Ash'ari response
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the three Quranic verses cited by the Mu'tazilites. Pay attention to how the Ash'ari response begins with 'قلنا' (we said).
The verses are: 49:2, 9:17, and 2:264.
2
Identify the Ash'ari reinterpretation
The Ash'aris explain that these verses refer to deeds done in a blameworthy manner (e.g., charity with reproach), not to the cancellation of prior rewards. They give the example of charity with and without reproach.
3
Understand the undisputed case
The Ash'aris concede that nullification by apostasy (disbelief) is true, but that is not what the debate is about. This shows they accept some form of nullification.
The text says: 'فليس من المتنازع في شيء' (that is not part of the dispute).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الإحباط مصرح في التنزيل كقوله تعالى : ولا تجهروا له بالقول كجهر بعضكم لبعض أن تحبط أعمالكم وأولئك حبطت أعمالهم ولا تبطلوا صدقاتكم بالمن والأذى قلنا : لا بالمعنى الذي قصد، ثم بل بمعنى أن من عمل عملاً صالحاً استحق به الذم وكان يمكنه أن يعمله على وجه يستحق به المدح والثواب، يقال إنه أحبط عمله كالصدقة مع المن والأذى وبدونهما. وأما إحباط الطاعات بالكفر بمعنى أنه لا يثاب عليها البتة. فليس من المتنازع في شيء، وحين تنبه أبو علي وأبو هاشم لفساد هذا الرأي رجعا عن التمادي بعض الرجوع . فقالا : إن المعاصي إنما تحبط الطاعات إذا أربت عليها . وإن أربت الطاعات أحبطت المعاصي . ثم ليس النظر إلى أعداد الطاعات والمعاصي بل إلى مقادير الأوزار ، ولا وجود فرب كبيرة يغلب وزرها أجور طاعات كثيرة، ولا سبيل إلى
They said: Nullification is explicitly mentioned in the Revelation, such as His statement: 'and do not raise your voices above the voice of the Prophet, lest your deeds become nullified' and 'those are the ones whose deeds have become nullified' and 'do not invalidate your charities by reproach and injury.' We said: No, not in the sense they intended. Rather, it means that one who does a good deed deserving of blame, when he could have done it in a way deserving praise and reward, it is said he nullified his deed, like charity with reproach and injury versus without them. As for the nullification of acts of obedience by disbelief, meaning that one is not rewarded for them at all, that is not part of the dispute. And when Abu Ali and Abu Hashim became aware of the corruption of this view, they partially retreated. They said: Sins only nullify acts of obedience if they outweigh them. And if acts of obedience outweigh, they nullify sins. Then the consideration is not of the numbers of acts of obedience and sins, but of the weights of burdens. And sometimes a major sin outweighs the rewards of many acts of obedience. And there is no way to...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite scriptural argument for ihbat and the Ash'ari response, including a notable partial retraction by two leading Mu'tazilites. The passage begins with the Mu'tazilite objection: the Quran explicitly mentions nullification of deeds, citing verses about raising voices (49:2), those whose deeds are nullified (9:17), and invalidating charity by reproach (2:264). The Ash'ari response distinguishes the intended meaning: these verses refer to deeds done in a blameworthy manner (e.g., charity with reproach), not to the cancellation of prior rewards by a later sin. The only real nullification is by apostasy, which is not disputed. The author then notes that Abu Ali al-Jubbai and Abu Hashim al-Jubbai, leading Mu'tazilites, themselves recognized the weakness of the extreme ihbat view and partially retracted. They revised their position: nullification occurs only when sins outweigh good deeds, and similarly good deeds can nullify sins. Moreover, they shifted from counting acts to weighing their significance. This shows internal Mu'tazilite disagreement and a move toward a more nuanced position.

Key Terms

تنزيل

أذى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris deny all Quranic references to nullification of deeds.
Correction: The Ash'aris accept the verses but reinterpret them as referring to deeds done in a blameworthy manner, not the cancellation of prior rewards.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'aris reinterpret the Quranic verse about invalidating charity (2:264)?
A) They consider it abrogated.
B) They say it refers to charity done with reproach, not the cancellation of past rewards.
C) They apply it only to obligatory charity.
D) They deny it applies to believers.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarīs affirm Quranic nullification but restrict it to deeds done in a blameworthy manner, not the cancelation of prior good deeds by later sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you give charity or perform any good deed, do you check your intention (niyyah) for hidden motives like seeking praise or feeling superior? What blameworthy attitudes might be creeping into your worship without you realizing it? Today, pick one good deed and consciously eliminate any reproach (mann) or injury (adhā) from it—even in your heart.
?
This lesson shows that even a good deed done in a blameworthy manner can be 'nullified' in the sense that it becomes defective. Think of a time you did something good but with an impure motive. How did that affect your sense of spiritual reward? What will you do differently tomorrow to ensure your deeds are both outwardly correct and inwardly pure?
?
The Ash'aris distinguish apostasy from other sins regarding nullification. Consider the gravity of disbelief: if 'kufr' can undo all good deeds, how seriously do you guard your faith? Are there any doubts, habits, or companions that are slowly weakening your conviction? Write down one concrete step you can take this week to strengthen your imān and protect it from nullification.

The Muʿtazilite Partial Retraction: The Weighing Principle
Mu'tazilite partial retraction: weighing principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muʿtazilite revision of iḥbāṭ: that nullification depends on the weight of sins versus good deeds, not on mere numbers.

Core Idea
Sins and good deeds cancel each other only if one outweighs the other, and weights matter more than counts.

Two leading Muʿtazilites, Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī and his son Abū Hāshim, recognized the weakness of the extreme iḥbāṭ view. They partially retreated, adopting a principle of weighing: sins nullify good deeds only if they outweigh them, and vice versa. Moreover, they shifted focus from counting actions to evaluating their magnitude. This internal reform shows that even within Muʿtazilism, the doctrine evolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the retraction text
Read the Arabic passage noting that Abu Ali and Abu Hashim are the subjects. They 'partially retreated.'
The text says: 'وحين تنبه أبو علي وأبو هاشم لفساد هذا الرأي رجعا عن التمادي بعض الرجوع'
2
Identify the two principles
First, nullification occurs only if sins outweigh good deeds (or vice versa). Second, the criterion is the weight (magnitude) of deeds, not their number.
'إن المعاصي إنما تحبط الطاعات إذا أربت عليها' and 'ليس النظر إلى أعداد الطاعات والمعاصي بل إلى مقادير الأوزار'
3
Reflect on the shift
Contrast this with the original Mu'tazilite view that any major sin nullifies all good deeds. This revision significantly limits the scope of nullification, bringing it closer to Sunni concepts of justice and mercy.

Iḥbāṭ and Muwāzana: Two Mu'tazilite Views
Sanusi contrasts Abu Ali's pure nullification with Abu Hashim's balancing model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the pure nullification (al-iḥbāṭ al-maḥḍ) of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and the balancing (al-muwāzana) of Abu Hashim regarding the cancellation of reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites disagreed on how sins cancel rewards: pure nullification vs. balancing with compensation.

The Mu'tazilites, known for their rational theology, debated how acts of obedience and disobedience affect each other. This passage by Sanusi presents the two main positions: Abu Ali's view that the lesser is fully cancelled, and Abu Hashim's view that only an equivalent portion is cancelled. Understanding their difference is key to grasping later Islamic theological discussions on divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own structure come through before interpreting.
Notice the stray word 'رجح' at the end—likely a marginal note.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the definitions of Abu Ali's 'pure nullification' and Abu Hashim's 'balancing'. Pay attention to the examples given for each.
The examples use parts of reward and punishment to illustrate the effect.
3
Clarify the underlying disagreement
The passage ends by noting a difference of opinion on what exactly is being balanced: acts, merits, or entitlements. Understand how Abu Ali and Abu Hashim differed on this point.
This nuance is crucial for accurate understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضبط ذلك، بل هو مفوض إلى علم الله، ثم افترقا فزعم أبو علي أن الأقل يسقط، ولا يسقط من الأكثر شيئاً، وسقوط الأقل يكون عقاباً إذا كان الساقط ثواباً، وثواباً إذا كان الساقط عقاباً. وهذا هو الإحباط المحض. وقال أبو هاشم: الأقل يسقط. ويسقط من الأكثر ما يقابله. مثلاً: له مائة جزء من العقاب واكتسب ألف جزء من الثواب فإنه يسقط عنه العقاب ومائة جزء من الثواب بمقابلته ويبقى له تسعمائة جزء من الثواب. ومن له مائة جزء من الثواب واكتسب ألفاً من العقاب، سقط ثوابه ومائة جزء من عقابه. وهذا هو القول بالموازنة، لا ما قال في المواقف إنه يوازن بين الطاعات والمعاصي، فأيهما أحبط الآخر، واختلفت كلمتهم في أن الإحباط والموازنة بين الفعلين أعني الطاعة والمعصية، أو المستحقين أعني الثواب والعقاب أو الاستحقاقين، مال الجبائي إلى الأول وأبو هاشم إلى الثاني، وهو المختار عند الأكثرين. رجح.
He restricted that, rather it is delegated to the knowledge of God. Then they differed, so Abu Ali claimed that the lesser is nullified, and nothing is nullified from the greater, and the nullification of the lesser is punishment if what is nullified was reward, and reward if what is nullified was punishment. That is pure nullification (al-iḥbāṭ al-maḥḍ). And Abu Hashim said: The lesser is nullified, and what corresponds to it from the greater is nullified. For example: he has one hundred parts of punishment and acquires one thousand parts of reward, so his punishment is nullified and one hundred parts of reward by way of compensation, and nine hundred parts of reward remain for him. And he who has one hundred parts of reward and acquires one thousand parts of punishment, his reward is nullified and one hundred parts of his punishment. This is the doctrine of balancing (al-muwāzana), not what was said in al-Mawāqif that he balances between acts of obedience and sins, whichever of the two nullifies the other. And their word differed concerning whether al-iḥbāṭ and al-muwāzana are between the two acts, i.e., obedience and sin, or between the two merits (mustaḥiqqayn), i.e., reward and punishment, or between the two entitlements (istiḥqāqayn). Al-Jubba'i inclined to the first, and Abu Hashim to the second, and it is the chosen view of the majority. Rajaha.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the two main Mu'tazilite positions on the nullification of reward by sin and vice versa. It begins by affirming that the precise manner of such nullification is not subject to rational determination but is delegated to God's knowledge. Then it presents the two views: Abu Ali al-Jubba'i held that when a lesser amount (of either reward or punishment) is opposed by a greater amount of the opposite, the lesser is completely nullified and nothing of the greater is lost—this is 'pure nullification' (al-iḥbāṭ al-maḥḍ). For example, a small reward is cancelled by a large sin, becoming punishment, and vice versa. Abu Hashim, on the other hand, held that the lesser is nullified, but an equivalent portion of the greater is also nullified—this is 'balancing' (al-muwāzana). The text gives concrete examples. It then clarifies that this balancing is not the same as later formulations in al-Mawāqif, which speak of balancing acts as such. The passage also notes a disagreement among the Mu'tazila about what exactly is being balanced: the acts themselves (obedience and disobedience), the personal merits (the doers), or the entitlements (the reward and punishment). Al-Jubba'i preferred the first, Abu Hashim the second, which became the prevalent view. The stray word 'رجح' at the end is likely a textual residue, possibly meaning 'he preferred' or a marginal note.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الموازنة
الثواب
العقاب
الاستحقاق
أبو علي الجبائي
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muwāzana means balancing acts of obedience and sin directly.
Correction: Abu Hashim's muwāzana balances the entitlements (reward and punishment) not the acts themselves.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, what happens when a lesser amount of reward is opposed by a greater amount of punishment?
A) The greater is partially nullified.
B) The lesser is nullified and becomes punishment, with nothing lost from the greater.
C) Both are completely nullified.
D) The lesser remains but is outweighed.

Key Takeaway
Abu Ali and Abu Hashim offered two distinct Mu'tazilite models for how reward and punishment cancel each other: complete nullification of the lesser vs. proportional balancing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own deeds, do you ever feel overwhelmed by your shortcomings? How does the idea that the exact balancing of reward and punishment is known only to God—not to you—change your anxiety about your spiritual state?
?
Which of these two Mu'tazilite views (pure nullification or balancing) resonates more with your sense of God's justice? Does your preference reveal something about your own expectations of how God should treat you?
?
If you truly believed that every sin cancels some of your reward (as in Abu Hashim's model), would you live differently today? What specific action would you take to 'tip the scales' in your favor?

Al-Razi's Refutation of Nullification Without Loss
Al-Razi refutes nullification with rational and scriptural arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's rational and scriptural arguments against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of nullification without partial loss.

Core Idea
Nullification without loss is unjust and contradicts the Quranic promise of reward.

This passage presents Imam al-Razi's first refutation of the Mu‘tazilite view that a greater quantity of reward or punishment can entirely nullify a lesser one without any loss to itself. He argues that such annihilation of the lesser is both rationally unjust and scripturally unsupported. The argument targets Abu Ali's version of pure nullification, where the greater cancels the lesser entirely without losing any of its own value.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the two-part structure: rational argument followed by scriptural evidence.
The passage is concise—pay attention to each clause.
2
Identify the two arguments
Al-Razi gives a rational argument (injustice and symmetry) then a scriptural argument (Quranic verse). Find both and see how they connect.
3
Understand the rational argument
Why does al-Razi say nullification without loss is unjust? Why does he say it is not more appropriate for the new event to cancel the old than for the old to repel the new?
This is the core of the rational critique.
4
Connect to scripture
The verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' promises reward for even the smallest good. How does this contradict nullification?
The verse implies that no good deed can be entirely annihilated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة لا يخفي على أحد أن القول بما ذهبا إليه من الإحباط والموازنة لا يصح إلا بنص من الشارع صريح، ونقل صحيح. واستدل الإمام الرازي على بطلانه بأن الأكثر إذا أحبط الأقل، فإن لم يحبط منه شيء، كما هو رأي أبي علي صارت الطاعة السابقة لغواً محضاً لا تجلب نفعاً ولا تدفع ضراً، وهو باطل إما عقلاً فلكونه ظلماً، ولأنه ليس انتفاء الباقي بطرآن الحادث أولى من اندفاع الحادث بوجود الباقي. وإما سمعاً فلقوله تعالى: ﴿ فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره ﴾ وغير ذلك.
And in summary, it is not hidden from anyone that the doctrine to which they both went, of nullification (al-iḥbāṭ) and balancing (al-muwāzana), is not valid except by an explicit text from the Lawgiver and a sound transmission. Imam al-Razi argued for its invalidity by [saying]: if the greater nullifies the lesser, and nothing is nullified from it, as is the view of Abu Ali, then the prior act of obedience becomes pure futility, bringing no benefit and repelling no harm. That is invalid: rationally, because it is injustice, and because the non-existence of the remaining [priority] by the occurrence of the new [event] is not more appropriate than the repulsion of the new [event] by the existence of the remaining [priority]; and scripturally, because of His saying, Exalted: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' and other [verses].
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage begins al-Razi's refutation of both Mu'tazilite positions (al-iḥbāṭ and al-muwāzana) by noting that such doctrines require explicit scriptural proof, which is lacking. It then presents the first specific argument against Abu Ali's version of pure nullification. The argument proceeds in two steps. First, if a greater quantity (of reward or punishment) nullifies a lesser one entirely without any loss to the greater, then the previous act (the one represented by the lesser quantity) becomes utterly pointless—it yields no benefit and wards off no harm. This is unacceptable. Al-Razi then gives two reasons for its invalidity: rational and scriptural. Rationally, it is injustice (ẓulm) to nullify a legitimate entitlement without compensation. Moreover, it is no more justified that the prior entitlement should cease because of a new event than that the new event should be repelled by the persistence of the prior entitlement—there is no rational basis to prefer one over the other. Scripturally, God's words in Surat al-Zalzala (99:7) promise that even an atom's weight of good will be seen (rewarded), which directly contradicts the idea that it can be entirely nullified. Other verses also support this. The argument is concise: if the greater cancels the lesser without affecting itself, the lesser's value is annihilated, which is both unjust and contrary to revelation.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الموازنة
الظلم
السمع
العقل
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rational arguments are unnecessary for matters of reward and punishment; scripture alone suffices.
Correction: Al-Razi uses both reason and scripture: reason shows injustice, and scripture contradicts nullification.

Checkpoint
What is the rational argument al-Razi gives against Abu Ali's view that the greater nullifies the lesser without loss to itself?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It is unjust and there is no reason to prefer the cessation of the prior over the repulsion of the new.
C) It leads to an infinite regress.
D) It is impossible for one thing to be nullified by another.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes nullification by arguing it is both unjust and contradicts the Quranic promise of reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a small good deed you did didn't matter because of your many sins? How does al-Razi's argument and the verse 'an atom's weight of good will be seen' confront that feeling right now? What will you do today to act on that truth?
?
When you consider your own life, do you treat your good deeds as 'futile' if they are not large? How does the rational argument that nullification without loss is 'injustice' change how you view God's fairness toward your own efforts?
?
If you truly believed that every single good deed, no matter how small, is preserved and rewarded by God, what small act of obedience would you start doing consistently starting today? What is holding you back?

Al-Razi's Three Arguments Against Balancing
Al-Razi presents three logical refutations of balancing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's three logical arguments against Abu Hashim's theory of balancing entitlements (al-muwāzanah) and the reason each leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Balancing entitlements leads to circularity, infinite annihilation, and a paradox of mutual removal.

Abu Hashim proposed that when a person has both reward and punishment (from different acts), the greater entitlement is reduced by an amount equal to the smaller. Al-Razi rejects this with three logical objections. These arguments use basic rational principles—circularity, symmetry of parts, and the impossibility of mutual cancellation—to show that the balancing theory is internally inconsistent. Read on to see how each objection dismantles the theory step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that Al-Razi presents three distinct objections, each introduced with 'أما أولاً' (first), 'أما ثانياً' (second), and 'أما ثالثاً' (third).
The passage is dense; take your time with each argument.
2
Examine the first argument: circularity (al-dawr)
Focus on the sentence beginning with 'أما أولاً'. Understand why Al-Razi claims that if the prior entitlement's removal is caused by the new one's occurrence, and the new one's occurrence requires the prior's removal, then we have circular reasoning.
Circularity makes a valid causal explanation impossible.
3
Examine the second argument: equal parts lead to total annihilation
Read the 'أما ثانياً' section and the example from al-Muḥaṣṣal. Why does Al-Razi argue that the small entitlement would annihilate all of the large one?
The key is the principle that all parts of reward are equal, so the small punishment cannot selectively cancel only some.
4
Examine the third argument: paradox of simultaneous or sequential removal
Read the 'أما ثالثاً' section. Al-Razi considers two possibilities: both entitlements are removed at once (impossible) or one after the other (also impossible). Follow his reasoning to see the paradox.
This argument shows that mutual removal leads to a logical dead end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن حبط من الأكثر ما يوازن الأقل كما هو رأي أبي هاشم فباطل أيضاً. أما أولاً فلأنهما لما كانا متنافيين كان طرآن الحادث مشروطاً بزوال السابق. فلو كان زواله لأجل طرآن الحادث، لزم الدور. وأما ثانياً فلأن تأثير ذلك الاستحقاق القليل في بعض أجزاء الكثير ليس أولى من تأثيره في الباقي، لكون الأجزاء متساوية. وحينئذ يلزم أن يفنى بذلك القليل كل ذلك الكثير. وهو باطل وفاقاً. وهذا ما قال في المحصل أنه إذا استحق بالطاعة عشرة أجزاء من الثواب وبالمعصية خمسة أجزاء من العقاب، فليس انتفاء استحقاق إحدى الخمستين أولى من انتفاء استحقاق الخمسة الأخرى، لتساوي أجزاء الثواب واستحقاقاتها. وأما ثالثاً فلأن زوال كل من الاستحقاقين بالآخر إما أن يكون دفعة، وهو محال، لأنه إذا كان عدم كل منهما لوجود الآخر، فلو عدما دفعة لوجدا دفعة. ولكن العلة موجودة حال حدوث المعلول، فيلزم كونهما موجودين حال كونهما معدومين. وإما أن لا يكون دفعة وهو أيضاً باطل لأنه إذا كان سبب زوال الأول حدوث الثاني، فما لم يوجد الثاني، لا يزول الأول، وإذا وجد الثاني وزال الأول، استحال زوال الثاني لأنه لا مزيل له، لأن التقدير أن كلا منهما إنما يزول بالآخر، وهذا ما يقال إن الثاني كان قاصراً عن الغلبة حين ما لم يكن مغلوباً، فكيف إذا صار مغلوباً.
And if what balances the lesser is nullified from the greater, as is the view of Abu Hashim, it is also invalid. First: because since the two are mutually exclusive, the occurrence of the new [entitlement] is conditioned by the removal of the previous. So if its removal is due to the occurrence of the new, circularity (al-dawr) follows. Second: because the effect of that small entitlement on some parts of the many is not more appropriate than its effect on the rest, since the parts are equal. Then it would follow that that small [entitlement] annihilates all of that much [entitlement]. That is invalid by consensus. This is what was said in al-Muḥaṣṣal: if one has earned ten parts of reward and five parts of punishment, the non-existence of the entitlement of one of the two fives is not more appropriate than the non-existence of the entitlement of the other five, due to the equality of the parts of reward and their entitlements. And third: because the removal of each of the two entitlements by the other is either simultaneous, which is impossible, because if the non-existence of each is due to the existence of the other, then if both become non-existent simultaneously, they would both exist simultaneously. But the cause exists at the moment of the effect's occurrence, so they would have to be existent at the moment they are non-existent. Or it is not simultaneous, and that is also invalid, because if the cause of the removal of the first is the occurrence of the second, then as long as the second has not occurred, the first does not cease; and when the second occurs and the first ceases, the removal of the second becomes impossible because there is no remover for it, since the supposition is that each is only removed by the other. This is what is said: the second was incapable of overcoming when it was not overcome, so how [can it overcome] when it has become overcome?
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's three arguments against Abu Hashim's balancing view, where from the greater an amount equal to the lesser is nullified. The first argument is circularity (al-dawr): if the greater and lesser entitlements are mutually exclusive, the occurrence of the new one requires the removal of the prior one. If we say the prior's removal is caused by the new one's occurrence, that is circular because the new one's occurrence itself depends on the prior's removal. The second argument appeals to the symmetry of parts: if one has many equal parts of reward, and a smaller number of punishment (say five punishment against ten reward), the effect of the small punishment on any specific part of the reward is no more likely than on any other. Therefore, the small amount would have to annihilate all the reward, which is absurd by consensus. The text references al-Razi's work al-Muḥaṣṣal, giving the example of ten parts of reward and five of punishment. The third argument considers two possibilities for mutual removal: either both are removed simultaneously, or one after the other. Simultaneous removal is impossible because if each's non-existence is due to the other's existence, then both would have to exist and not exist at the same time. Non-simultaneous removal is also impossible: if the first is removed only when the second occurs, then after the second occurs and the first is gone, the second cannot be removed because it would need a remover, but the only supposed remover (the first) no longer exists. Thus the second, which was initially weaker, cannot now overcome when it is already victorious—a paradox. These arguments are designed to show logical inconsistency in the balancing theory.

Key Terms
الدور
الاستحقاق
الأجزاء المتساوية
المحصل
الزوال دفعة
الزوال تدريجياً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Balancing entitlements is a simple arithmetic cancellation of equal parts.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that balancing leads to logical contradictions (circularity, total annihilation, paradox of removal), so it cannot be a straightforward arithmetic operation.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's first argument against Abu Hashim's balancing theory?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It requires that the small amount annihilates all of the large amount.
C) It leads to circularity (al-dawr).
D) It implies simultaneous existence and non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's three arguments show that the balancing theory of entitlements is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own deeds, do you ever subconsciously assume that your good deeds will simply cancel out your bad deeds, allowing you to be careless about small sins? How does Al-Razi's argument challenge that assumption and demand you to see each deed as carrying its own weight?
?
The third argument reveals that two opposing forces cannot remove each other without logical impossibility. In your life, are there conflicting desires or commitments that you try to keep balanced, hoping they will neutralize each other? What would it mean for you to face them individually instead of relying on their mutual cancellation?
?
Al-Razi's proof shows that divine justice is far more precise than a simple arithmetic balance. How does this truth make you reconsider your accountability before Allah? Will you continue to treat any of your actions as negligible, or will you now regard each as significant and permanent?

Objection and Answer: Persistence vs. Newness
Objection and answer on the relative strength of new vs. prior entitlement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the persistence of prior entitlement against the objection that new acts are stronger.

Core Idea
Persistence strengthens entitlement even when the cause is no longer present.

This lesson presents a key objection in the debate about nullification and balancing of deeds. Opponents argue that newly acquired reward or punishment is stronger because it is accompanied by its cause. The author responds by emphasizing the enduring strength of prior entitlements, introducing the principle that prevention is easier than removal. This passage clarifies a subtle point in the theology of divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims the new act is stronger because its cause is present. Note the two scenarios: nullification (complete replacement) and balancing (partial offset).
2
Analyze the author's answer
The answer denies the premise and argues that prior entitlement is stronger due to persistence. Understand the principle 'prevention is easier than removal' (al-dafʿ ahyanu min al-rafʿ).
This principle is key in Islamic legal theory.
3
Examine the final condition
The author notes that even if the objection held, it only applies when the new act is greater in magnitude. Consider the exceptions mentioned: when the prior is greater or when amounts are reversed.
This limits the objection's scope and shows nuanced reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بوجوه : الأول - أن الطاريء أقوى وبالبقاء أولى، لكونه مقارناً لمؤثره، الذي يوجده، بخلاف السابق فإنه وإن كان موجوداً، لكن لم يبق معه مؤثره، فإذاً يجوز على الإحباط أن يفني السابق بالطاريء ويبقى هو بحاله وعلى الموازنة، أن يفنى من الطاريء ما يقابل السابق. ثم يفنى السابق بما بقي من الطارىء. والجواب : المنع، بل السابق لاستمرار وجوده وتحقق علة بقائه أقوى وأبقى. والطارىء لقربه من العدم وعدم تحقق علة بقائه بالفناء أولى على أن الدفع أهون من الرفع ثم هذا - على تقدير صحته - إنما يتأتى فيما إذا كان الأكثر طارئاً بخلاف ما إذا استحق بالطاعة ثواباً كثيراً، أو بالمعصية عقاباً أقل، أو بالعكس.
And he objected with [several] points: First: that the new [act] is stronger and more deserving of remaining, because it is accompanied by its cause, which brings it into existence, unlike the prior, for although it exists, its cause no longer remains with it. Therefore, it is possible in [the doctrine of] nullification (al-iḥbāṭ) that the prior is annihilated by the new and the new remains as it is, and in [the doctrine of] balancing (al-muwāzana) that from the new what corresponds to the prior is annihilated, then the prior is annihilated by what remains of the new. The answer: denial; rather the prior, due to the continuity of its existence and the actualization of the cause of its persistence, is stronger and more lasting. The new, due to its proximity to non-existence and the non-actualization of the cause of its persistence, is more prone to annihilation, especially since repelling is easier than removing. Then this—assuming its validity—only applies when the greater is new, unlike the case where one has earned much reward by obedience, or little punishment by disobedience, or the opposite.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objection, introduced as one of several possible points, argues that the newly acquired entitlement (whether reward or punishment) is stronger than the pre-existing one. The reasoning is that the new act is accompanied by its efficient cause (mu'aththir) at the moment of its occurrence, whereas the cause that produced the prior act is no longer present. Hence, the prior entitlement is vulnerable to being overridden or annihilated by the new one, either in pure nullification (where the prior is completely replaced) or in balancing (where a portion of the new equal to the prior is deleted, and then the prior is deleted by the remainder). The answer (al-jawāb) denies this premise. It asserts that the prior entitlement is actually stronger and more lasting because its existence has endured and it has an established cause that continues to sustain it. The new entitlement, being recent, is closer to non-existence and lacks a confirmed cause for its persistence; it is more liable to be annihilated. The answer adds a general principle: prevention (dafʿ) is easier than removal (rafʿ). Finally, the answer notes that even if the objection were valid, it would only apply when the greater quantity is the new one, not when the greater is pre-existing or when the quantities are reversed. This nuanced conclusion restricts the force of the objection.

Key Terms
الطارئ
السابق
المؤثر
علة البقاء
الدفع
الرفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Newly acquired reward or punishment is always stronger than prior entitlement because its cause is present.
Correction: Persistence over time establishes a cause of continuation that makes prior entitlement stronger, and prevention is easier than removal.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the objection?
A) The new act is always stronger.
B) The prior act is stronger because its existence has persisted and its cause remains effective.
C) Neither is stronger; both are equal.
D) The objection is valid but only in cases of minor sins.

Key Takeaway
The prior entitlement is stronger due to persistence, and prevention (dafʿ) is easier than removal (rafʿ).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good deed you performed a long time ago. Do you still count on its reward, or has its significance faded in your mind? How does the lesson's emphasis on the strength of prior entitlement challenge you to renew your confidence in Allah's justice?
?
When you commit a recent sin, do you feel it overpowers your past good deeds? How does the principle that 'repelling is easier than removing' change your approach to avoiding sin today?
?
Are there acts of worship you have stopped because you think their effect has worn off? Reflect on the statement that the prior is stronger due to continuity. What one action will you recommit to consistently this week?

Consensus on Forgiveness and the Main Disagreement
Enumeration of consensuses and the key disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three points of consensus regarding forgiveness of sins and the key disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila on forgiving major sins without repentance.

Core Idea
Major sins after repentance are forgiven; the debate is about forgiveness without it.

Before we examine the arguments, we need a clear map of where all Muslims agree and where they part ways. The passage begins by stating three points of consensus: minor sins always forgiven, major sins after repentance, no forgiveness for disbelief (though rational possibility is debated). Then it highlights the core disagreement: can major sins be forgiven without repentance? The Ash'aris affirm it; the Mu'tazila deny it by revelation, even if rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on consensus and disagreement
Read the Arabic text from 'اتفقت الأمة' to 'عند الأكثرين منهم' and its translation. Note the three consensuses and the one disagreement.
The text uses 'اتفقت الأمة' for consensus and 'إختلفوا' for disagreement.
2
Identify the three points of consensus
List the three points: (1) minor sins forgiven absolutely, (2) major sins forgiven after repentance, (3) no forgiveness for disbelief (with rational disagreement).
3
Understand the main disagreement
The disagreement is about forgiveness of major sins without repentance. Ash'aris say yes (and affirm its occurrence), Mu'tazila say no based on revelation, though most Mu'tazila consider it rationally possible.
See how both sides agree on rational possibility but differ on revealed evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني عشر - اتفقت الأمة على العفو عن الصغائر مطلقاً، وعن الكبائر بعد التوبة، وعلى أنه لا عفو عن الكفر على اختلاف في الجواز عقلاً، وإختلفوا في العفو عن الكبائر بدون التوبة، فجوزه أصحابنا، بل أثبتوه ، ومنعه المعتزلة سمعاً، وإن جاز عقلاً عند الأكثرين منهم . لنا على الجواز أن العقاب حقه . فلـه إسقاطه . وعلى الوقوع النصوص الناطقة : ويعفو عن السيئات (۱) ويعفو عن كثير ) (۲) إن الله يغفر الذنوب جميعاً (۳) ( إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء ) (٤) وفي الأحاديث أيضاً كثرة والتخصيص بالصغائر أو بما بعد التوبة أو الحمل على تأخير العقوبات المستحقة، أو عدم شرع الحدود في غاية المعاصي، أو على ترك وضع الآصار عليهم. والفضائح في الدنيا، مع كونه عدولاً عن الظاهر بلا دليل ومخالفة لأقوال المفسرين وللأحاديث الصحيحة الصريحة مما لا يصح في البعض، إذ المغفرة بالتوبة لا يخص ما دون الشرك، ولا يلائم التعليق بالمشيئة وباقي المعاني لا يناسب النفي عن الشرك . مطلقاً، وعن ونطق الكتاب والسنة بأن الله تعالى عفو غفور يعفو عن الصغائر الكبائر بعد التوبة، ولا يعفو عن الكفر قطعاً، وإن جاز عقلاً، ومنع بعضهم الجواز
He said: The twelfth topic - The community agreed on forgiveness of minor sins absolutely, and of major sins after repentance, and that there is no forgiveness of disbelief with a difference of opinion on its permissibility rationally. And they disagreed on forgiveness of major sins without repentance. Our companions permitted it, rather affirmed it, and the Mu‘tazila denied it by revelation, though it is permissible rationally according to most of them. Our proof for permissibility is that punishment is His right, so He may drop it. And for occurrence [there are] the speaking texts: 'And He forgives the evil deeds' (1) and 'And He forgives much' (2) 'Indeed Allah forgives all sins' (3) 'Indeed Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4). And there are many hadiths as well. And specifying [them] to minor sins or to after repentance or interpreting them as deferral of deserved punishments or as non-legislation of hadd penalties for the worst sins or as removal of burdens from them and disgrace in this world, even though it is a departure from the apparent meaning without evidence and contradicts the statements of the commentators and the authentic explicit hadiths—this is not valid in some [cases], because forgiveness through repentance is not specific to what is less than shirk, and the conditional clause with will does not harmonize, and the other interpretations do not suit the negation of shirk. Absolutely, and the Book and Sunnah declare that Allah Almighty is Pardoning and Forgiving, He forgives minor and major sins after repentance, and does not forgive disbelief decisively, even if permissible rationally, and some denied the permissibility.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage introduces the twelfth topic (al-mabḥath al-thāniy ʿashar) concerning forgiveness of sins. It first states points of consensus: the entire Muslim community agrees that minor sins are forgiven absolutely, that major sins are forgiven after repentance, and that disbelief is not forgiven—though there is disagreement on whether it is rationally possible for Allah to forgive disbelief. The main disagreement is over forgiveness of major sins without repentance. The Ash‘aris (our companions) affirm it and even assert its occurrence, while the Mu‘tazila deny it based on revelation (sam‘an), even though most of them admit it is rationally possible. The Ash‘ari proof for permissibility is straightforward: punishment is Allah's right, so He may waive it. For actual occurrence, they adduce Quranic verses such as 'He forgives the evil deeds', 'He forgives much', 'Allah forgives all sins', and 'Allah does not forgive association with Him and forgives what is less than that for whom He wills'. The Mu‘tazila try to restrict these verses to minor sins or to forgiveness after repentance, or interpret them as postponement of punishment or as non-imposition of legal penalties. The author refutes these interpretations: they are arbitrary departures from the apparent meaning without evidence, contradict the commentators and authentic hadiths, and are inconsistent (e.g., the verse about forgiveness after repentance is not specific to less-than-shirk, and the conditional 'for whom He wills' does not fit forgiveness only after repentance). The author concludes that the Book and Sunnah affirm that Allah forgives minor and major sins after repentance and does not forgive disbelief absolutely, though some even deny the rational possibility of forgiving disbelief.

Key Terms
عفو
صغائر
كبائر
توبة
كفر
مشيئة
معتزلة
أصحابنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that forgiveness of major sins without repentance is rationally impossible.
Correction: Both Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila agree it is rationally possible; the disagreement is about whether it actually occurs based on revelation.

Checkpoint
On what basis do the Mu'tazila deny forgiveness of major sins without repentance?
A) They consider it rationally impossible.
B) They deny it based on revelation (sam‘an).
C) They believe it contradicts justice.
D) They interpret all forgiveness verses as referring only to minor sins.

Key Takeaway
The community agrees on three points about forgiveness, and the main dispute is whether major sins can be forgiven without repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila denied forgiveness without repentance because they feared it would encourage sin. Do you ever take God’s mercy for granted, continuing in sin while assuming He will forgive? How does this lesson challenge your attitude towards repentance?
?
The passage affirms that disbelief (kufr) is not forgiven. Is there any trace of disbelief in your heart—any doubt, arrogance, or attachment to falsehood? What must you do today to ensure your heart is pure from it?

Ash'ari Proofs and Refutation of Mu'tazila Interpretations
Proofs for permissibility and occurrence of forgiveness, and refutation of alternate interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proofs (rational and scriptural) for forgiveness of major sins without repentance and the refutation of Mu'tazila attempts to reinterpret the relevant verses.

Core Idea
Punishment is Allah's right; He may waive it, and scriptures affirm forgiveness.

After establishing the disagreement, the author provides arguments for the Ash'ari position and responds to Mu'tazila objections. The rational proof is simple: punishment is Allah's right, so He can cancel it. Then come the scriptural proofs from the Quran and hadith. The Mu'tazila try to restrict these verses by various interpretations; the author refutes each, showing they are arbitrary and inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof section
Read from 'لنا على الجواز' to the end of the Arabic passage. Note the rational proof first, then the scriptural proofs.
The rational proof: 'العقاب حقه فله إسقاطه'.
2
List the scriptural proofs
Find the Quranic verses cited: 'ويغفر السيئات', 'ويعفو عن كثير', 'إن الله يغفر الذنوب جميعاً', and 'إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء'.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila interpretations
The Mu'tazila interpret these verses as referring only to minor sins, or to after repentance, or as deferral of punishment, or as non-imposition of hadd penalties.
4
Study the refutation
The author argues these interpretations depart from the apparent meaning without evidence, contradict commentators and hadith, and are inconsistent (e.g., forgiveness after repentance not specific to less-than-shirk, and the conditional 'for whom He wills' does not fit only after repentance).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: The twelfth topic - The community agreed on forgiveness of minor sins absolutely, and of major sins after repentance, and that there is no forgiveness of disbelief with a difference of opinion on its permissibility rationally. And they disagreed on forgiveness of major sins without repentance. Our companions permitted it, rather affirmed it, and the Mu‘tazila denied it by revelation, though it is permissible rationally according to most of them. Our proof for permissibility is that punishment is His right, so He may drop it. And for occurrence [there are] the speaking texts: 'And He forgives the evil deeds' (1) and 'And He forgives much' (2) 'Indeed Allah forgives all sins' (3) 'Indeed Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4). And there are many hadiths as well. And specifying [them] to minor sins or to after repentance or interpreting them as deferral of deserved punishments or as non-legislation of hadd penalties for the worst sins or as removal of burdens from them and disgrace in this world, even though it is a departure from the apparent meaning without evidence and contradicts the statements of the commentators and the authentic explicit hadiths—this is not valid in some [cases], because forgiveness through repentance is not specific to what is less than shirk, and the conditional clause with will does not harmonize, and the other interpretations do not suit the negation of shirk. Absolutely, and the Book and Sunnah declare that Allah Almighty is Pardoning and Forgiving, He forgives minor and major sins after repentance, and does not forgive disbelief decisively, even if permissible rationally, and some denied the permissibility.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage introduces the twelfth topic (al-mabḥath al-thāniy ʿashar) concerning forgiveness of sins. It first states points of consensus: the entire Muslim community agrees that minor sins are forgiven absolutely, that major sins are forgiven after repentance, and that disbelief is not forgiven—though there is disagreement on whether it is rationally possible for Allah to forgive disbelief. The main disagreement is over forgiveness of major sins without repentance. The Ash‘aris (our companions) affirm it and even assert its occurrence, while the Mu‘tazila deny it based on revelation (sam‘an), even though most of them admit it is rationally possible. The Ash‘ari proof for permissibility is straightforward: punishment is Allah's right, so He may waive it. For actual occurrence, they adduce Quranic verses such as 'He forgives the evil deeds', 'He forgives much', 'Allah forgives all sins', and 'Allah does not forgive association with Him and forgives what is less than that for whom He wills'. The Mu‘tazila try to restrict these verses to minor sins or to forgiveness after repentance, or interpret them as postponement of punishment or as non-imposition of legal penalties. The author refutes these interpretations: they are arbitrary departures from the apparent meaning without evidence, contradict the commentators and authentic hadiths, and are inconsistent (e.g., the verse about forgiveness after repentance is not specific to less-than-shirk, and the conditional 'for whom He wills' does not fit forgiveness only after repentance). The author concludes that the Book and Sunnah affirm that Allah forgives minor and major sins after repentance and does not forgive disbelief absolutely, though some even deny the rational possibility of forgiving disbelief.

سيئات
مشيئة

A) Allah is all-merciful.
B) Punishment is Allah's right; He may drop it.
C) The Prophet intercedes for sinners.
D) Repentance is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris prove that Allah may forgive major sins without repentance, supported by reason and scripture, despite Mu'tazila reinterpretations.

What specific action can you take today to align your heart with the Ash'ari understanding that forgiveness is ultimately by Allah's will, not your own merit?
